conducted of reinforced earth slopes to evaluate if reinforced earth slopes are
economically viable for each area
C Subgrade Stabilization
The on site soils along the majority of the Palau Compact Road alignment generally
exist in a very moist to wet condition Based on our laboratory tests the recompacted
saprolite soils exhibit relatively low California Bearing Ratio CBR values of about 1 to 5
Strengthening of these subgrade soils may be required in certain areas in order to improve
the subsoil workability and to facilitate the project construction This may be accomplished
by the inclusion of additives such as lime or cement treatment and or incorporation of
ground stabilization fabrics at the subgrade level
1 Lime and or Cement Stabilization
Lime has been used to improve the properties of fine grained soils for many years
The general objectives of lime stabilization are to modify and improve the
characteristics of the soils thus expediting construction and improving the strength
and durability of the soils In particular it is desired to control the volume stability
swelling and shrinkage characteristics and to reduce the plasticity of the soils
When lime is added to clayey soils several chemical reactions are initiated
These reactions are cation exchange and flocculation agglomeration they are also
pozzolanic In the cation and flocculation agglomeration reactions the clay
particles tend to bond together to form larger sized particles These reactions tend
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
to decrease the liquid limit increase the plastic limit decrease the plasticity index
increase the workability and improve the strength and deformation properties of the
clay
The pozzolanic reactions involve the formation of cementitious materials between
lime and the silica and alumina of the clay The end product is a series of calcium
silicate hydrates It is important that a sufficient amount of lime be present to
provide a high environment In the long term the high pH causes a
breakdown of the crystal lattice of the clay and the formation of cementitious
products These reactions can continue for a long period of time
It should be noted that the presence of free sulfates in clay soils can be detrimental
With high sulfate content in the soil the initial stabilization may be satisfactory but
subsequent wetting can lead to expansion and breakdown of the cement structure
Attainment of a uniform and intimate mixture of lime with clay and proper curing in
the field are of vital importance and affect the quality of the end results
It is not anticipated that widespread use of lime for subgrade soil stabilization would
be implemented for this project especially since the quick lime needed for soil
stabilization is not readily available in the Republic of Palau and would need to be
specifically imported for this application However cement treatment may be
considered since cement also imported may be more readily available in the
Republic of Palau
The mechanism of cement stabilization is similar to that described above for lime
stabilization One of the more common uses of cement stabilization is for the
improvement of pumping subgrade soils Where a pumping subgrade condition is
encountered during compaction of the subgrade soils very moist saprolite soils
the pumping subgrade may be stabilized by cement treatment to reduce the
potential for pumping subgrade conditions Cement treatment of pumping
subgrade conditions may be applied to subgrade preparation and fill placement
As a preliminary guide one sack of cement 42 kg may be used for approximately
W O 3595 00 D Page 51
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
4 square meters of subgrade area This mix should be adjusted in the field based
on the exposed subgrade soil conditions
2 Ground Stabilization Fabrics
Construction on weak and or wet soils can cause delays and added material costs
In addition these types of soils cause rapid deterioration of the paved structures
resulting in significant maintenance costs during the life of the pavement structure
Construction fabrics have been used in recent years to provide a cost effective
solution where stabilization of the subgrade soils is needed in order to facilitate the
project construction and to enhance the long term performance of the pavement
structure Utilization of ground stabilization fabrics helps to solve these soil related
construction and maintenance problems and keep costs down in three ways
e unpaved roads and other areas when designed with ground stabilization
fabrics require less amount of aggregate
e construction schedules are more easily followed because work can continue
during most types of weather and
e ground stabilization fabrics protect the aggregate base course against
subgrade intrusion thus significantly reducing costly maintenance
The benefits resulting from the fabric functions of separation confinement and load
distribution are described below
a Separation
Ground stabilization fabrics with puncture and tear resistant properties act
as a separation barrier between the fine grained soils and load distributing
aggregate fill material As a separator it eliminates the loss of costly
aggregate material into the subgrade and prevents the upward pumping of
silt and other contaminating soil fines into the aggregate
b Confinement
Ground stabilization fabrics provide a high friction surface between the
subgrade and the aggregate layer that helps to keep the aggregate in place
This confining action maintains the thickness and hence the intended
load bearing capacity of the aggregate
W O 3595 00 D Page 52
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
Load Distribution
When placed between the subgrade and the aggregate layer ground
stabilization fabric with its high tensile strength and modulus acts to reduce
localized stress by redistributing traffic loads over a wider area of subgrade
In general these subgrade stabilization measures are more cost effective for higher
design traffic requiring thicker pavement sections For the lower design traffic it is
unlikely that ground stabilization fabrics would be cost effective to reduce the
thickness of the pavement structural section However ground stabilization fabrics
may be used as a means to facilitate earthwork construction in the wet
environment

Ideally the soft soils encountered within the limits of the proposed roadway
alignments should be removed and replaced with suitable backfill materials prior to new fill
placement and or pavement construction However due to the substantial depth and
lateral extent of the soft soil deposits anticipated in some of these areas complete removal
and replacement of the soft soil deposits would be very costly In addition some
construction difficulties may be anticipated because the use of heavy equipment may not
be feasible in these areas Therefore we believe that an alternate scheme utilizing soft
ground stabilization techniques may be more appropriate than complete removal and
replacement of the soft soil deposits prior to fill placement and or pavement construction
Complete removal and replacement of the soft soils should be considered where the
thickness of the soft soils is on the order of 1 to 2 meters or less in narrow valleys or
gullies and in areas where substantial fills will be placed on top of the drainage structures
which is further discussed in the Special Earthwork Requirements section of this report
Since the surface soils in the soft soil areas are marshy and wet we believe that it
would not have sufficient strength to support construction equipment Therefore a
working platform or stabilization layer should be established prior to filling of the area
The working platform may consist of a woven geotextile Mirafi 600X or equivalent or
geogrid or equivalent overlain by a layer of surge rock fill The woven
geotextile would eliminate the strict gradation requirements of the working platform since it
W O 3595 00 D G ABS AT Page 54
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
would act as a separator to prevent gravel loss into the soft ground and prevent the mud
and fine soil particles from contaminating the surge rock fill It also provides tensile
reinforcement to the surge rock fill and permits earlier use of heavy equipment on a
minimum thickness of surge rock fill A layer of geogrid would be more appropriate when
compared to the woven geotextile in areas where remnants from the clearing operations
may tear the woven geotextile The working platform should extend beyond the toe of the
fill slope for a sufficient distance to allow for construction of the fill embankment
In general the stabilization layer should consist of surge rock fill extending to a
minimum of 0 5 meters above the high water level or at least 0 75 meters in thickness
whichever is greater Where the thickness of the stabilization layer is greater than
1 meter we recommend that a layer of geogrid or equivalent spaced
at1 meter intervals be incorporated into the stabilization layer for additional
reinforcement A layer of non woven geotextile should be provided between the surge rock
fill and the upper general fill Graphic illustration of the stabilization layer is presented on
Plate 7 Soft Ground Stabilization
Due to the soft and yielding nature of the subsoils new fills should be placed in thin
level lifts at slow filling rates to reduce the possibility of shear failure in the soft material
It is estimated that the initial portion of the fill placement at the soft soil areas may proceed
at a rate of not more than 1 meter high per week The controlled rate of filling should be
maintained to allow the consolidating soils to gain sufficient strength to support the next lift
The actual filling rate in the soft soil areas should be established in the field based on
settlement gauge and settlement marker readings and adjusted to meet field conditions

F Causeway Fill
A portion of the proposed roadway alignment for the North Route will traverse a
bay in the northern part of the island of Babeldaob and will be constructed as a
causeway Therefore we envision that fill placement over water and the soft lagoonal
deposits between about and of the North Route alignment will
be required for construction of the proposed Ngaraard Causeway embankment A large
portion of the causeway will be flat with a finished crest elevation of about 1 8 meters
MSL Based on our field exploration and the plan and profile provided we anticipate fill
heights to be generally about 2 5 to 3 meters high with fills up to about 6 meters above
the existing mudline at the north abutment for the causeway bridge In general the
causeway embankment section should consist of the following layers from top to
bottom

We understand that it may be desirable to use clean granular fill to construct the
causeway from the mudline to above the water level in lieu of surge rock fill If granular

clean not more than 10 percent particles less than and free draining to

If a relatively clean and free draining granular fill is used to fill the portion of the
causeway fill below the water level it is possible to construct the causeway
embankment consisting entirely of granular fill in lieu of the surge rock fill Where a
clean and free draining granular fill is used in lieu of surge rock fill the layer of
non woven geotextile above the surge rock fill may be omitted

that the face of the causeway fill will be protected and the causeway will be constructed of

Based on the subsoil conditions encountered in our drilled borings along the
causeway alignment it appears that the causeway area is generally underlain by a thin
surficial crust overlying soft loose lagoonal deposits and rock formation at greater depths
Based on our engineering analyses settlements of the causeway fill on the order of about
300 to 600 mm may be anticipated along the causeway alignment It is estimated that
80 to 90 percent of the primary settlement should occur in about 8 to 12 months after the
causeway fill is placed For the north abutment settlements on the order of about 500 to
or more have been estimated with 80 to 90 percent of the primary settlements
occurring in about 6 to 8 months
In general placement of the causeway fills should be initiated as early as possible
to allow the existing ground to adjust to the new fill loads A settlement monitoring
program should be implemented to evaluate the magnitude and rate of the estimated
settlements during construction prior to installation of the pavement structural section and
improvements such as the causeway bridge and foundations on the causeway fills If the
estimated duration of settlement for the causeway fills need to be shortened to
accommodate the construction schedule a surcharge program may be considered to
accelerate the consolidation of the underlying soft loose subsoils in order to reduce the
settlement waiting period
Several circulation box culverts are planned within the causeway embankment
Considering that the causeway embankment may settle on the order of about 300 to
600 mm we believe that temporary drainage structures should be installed along the
causeway embankment in the interim during the causeway fill placement and settlement
waiting period for circulation purposes After the rate of settlement of the causeway
embankment has slowed down to an acceptable rate the causeway embankment may
then be excavated to remove the temporary drainage structures and installation of the
permanent circulation box culvert structures may commence

ground to raise the site to the proposed finished elevations These settlements will affect

the construction schedule and the earthwork quantity estimates for the project Based on

different surcharge loads to evaluate the settlement behavior of embankment fills
with varying thickness Based on the results of our laboratory tests and

4 Temporary Surcharge Fills
As previously indicated ground settlements due to fill placement over soft
compressible deposits are anticipated The settlement rates for the soft
compressible deposits would be much slower and would require longer settlement
waiting periods to reduce the effects of settlement on the improvements
constructed on fills The settlement waiting period for embankment fills constructed
over soft compressible deposits may take months to years depending on the nature
and thickness of the soft compressible deposit In order to reduce the settlement
waiting period and subsequent long term settlements a temporary surcharge
program may be considered for embankment fill over soft ground areas Surcharge
programs can aid in accelerating ground settlements thus reducing the settlement
waiting period Use of temporary surcharge fills to reduce the settlement waiting
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
period should be left up to the Contractor as an option However it should be
noted that potential slope instability of the fill embankment may be induced as a
result of improperly designed temporary surcharge fills Therefore details of the
temporary surcharge fill program should be submitted by the Contractor for review
and evaluation prior to implementation
Depending on the fill thickness and the nature of the soft compressible deposit a
30 day waiting period is generally recommended between completion of the
general fill or granular fill and the temporary surcharge fill placement This waiting
period is required to allow dissipation of excess pore pressures in order for the
subsoils to gain strength and to reduce excessive lateral deformation and possible
failure within the soft soils A slope ratio of up to may be used for the
temporary surcharge fill material on top of the fill The surcharge may be placed in
lifts of 1 meter high lightly compacted after the end of the 30 day waiting period
Adequate precautions must be taken to reduce erosion of the surcharge fill slopes
during construction and the subsequent surcharge period to attain the required
consolidation The actual settlement waiting period with the temporary surcharge
fill will be determined by settlement gauge or marker readings or vibrating wire
piezometers where appropriate as described in the previous subsection
H Erosion Control
Implementation of an adequate erosion control program would be crucial to the
satisfactory performance of the planned cut slopes and fill embankments against potential
failures In general erosion of the existing slopes planned cut slopes or new fill
embankments are caused by surface water run off General guidelines such as
establishment of vegetation and provision of interceptor drainage ditches and earth berms
have been provided and discussed under the Cut Slope Design and Fill Slope Design
subsections of this report However we understand that due to the inherent properties of
the on site soils establishment of vegetation at the slope face would be difficult and may
require a long period of time Therefore other erosion control methods or applications
such as Grouted Rubble Paving GRP and concrete lining may need to be considered as
part of the erosion control program of the proposed roadway project In addition to these
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
conventional rigid lining systems the use of flexible geosynthetic matting as a viable
solution for slope erosion control has become increasingly more acceptable and may be
considered for this project
Flexible geosynthetic matting provides a flexible ground armor that resists erosion
while allowing natural vegetation to establish Its primary and secondary functions include
the following 1 temporary erosion control 2 mulching 3 permanent erosion control
and 4 run off velocity reduction
1 Temporary Erosion Control
The flexibility weight web structure and porosity features of the flexible
geosynthetic matting make it particularly effective in temporary erosion control It
conforms easily to the ground surface and remains firmly in place as a stable
ground armor The mat shields soil surface from the erosive forces of wind and rain
and prevents soil seed and fertilizer from washing away Finally it provides a
rough surface to retain sediment deposits during run off
2 Mulching
The high porosity web structure of the flexible geosynthetic matting acts as a
non deteriorating mulch It holds soil seed and fertilizer in place and retains
sediment run off as a medium for root growth It also retains moisture and heat
necessary for germination and allows uninhibited growth of grass and other
vegetation
3 Permanent Erosion Control
Once vegetation growth is established the flexible geosynthetic matting continues
to provide permanent erosion control as it becomes embedded in the new stem and
root system It protects new vegetation from washout and reinforces and anchors
the vegetation in place as a cohesive unit
W O 3595 00 D GEALABE HAWAII Page 67
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
4 Run off Velocities Reduction
Revegetated slopes and ditches reinforced with the matting reduce run off flow
velocities which is an important advantage that cannot be achieved with concrete
lining It reduces run off flow volume and concentrations and reduces the need for
energy dissipation at the ditch outlet or slope base It also promotes water
percolation and ground water recharge
In order to provide the background soil data and parameters pertaining to the
design and application of the flexible geosynthetic matting and vegetative growth four soil
samples typical of the saprolite soils anticipated at the project site were selected for a
chemical soil analysis Results of the chemical soil analysis and _ preliminary
recommendations by Hawaiian Fertilizer Sales Inc are presented in Appendix C of this
report
1 Corrosion Potential
Resistivity is generally recognized as one of the most significant soil characteristics
with regard to the corrosivity of the soil In general the lower the resistivity the greater the
potential for corrosion of the buried metallic structure Conversely the higher the
resistivity the less likely the soil will contribute to corrosion The corrosion evaluation
parameters used for concrete pipes depend on the chlorides and sulfates found in the soil
In general the higher the amount of chlorides and sulfates present in the soil the greater
the potential for corrosion of the buried concrete structures Chemical analyses including
pH minimum resistivity chloride and sulfate were performed on a selected soil sample
obtained during our field exploration for corrosivity evaluation The results of these tests
are presented in Plate B 79 of Appendix B
J Shoreline Protection
In general we understand that an armor stone revetment over underlayer stone
along the slope face will be used for protection against the erosive forces of wave action at
the bridge abutments center pier and along the seaward side of the causeway
embankment For protection of the causeway fills along the landward side we
understand that either a graded stone riprap or an articulating block mat may be used
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
We recommend that a layer of woven armored erosion control fabric Mirafi 700X or
equivalent be placed underneath the graded stone riprap or articulating block mat on the
landward side and under the underlayer stone on the seaward side of the causeway
embankment The fabric serves to reduce the potential for the underlying fines from being
eroded away through the voids between the graded stones Placement of the woven
armored erosion control fabric below the graded stone riprap and underlayer stone should
be in accordance with the manufacturer s requirements It is also recommended that the
riprap and fabric layer be extended to appropriate depths below the level of scour
potential
K Pavement Design
In general we anticipate the vehicle loading for this project to be light and to consist
primarily of passenger vehicles light pick up trucks buses and occasional heavy trucks

L Connecting Roads
We understand that unpaved roads will be provided in some areas as connector

connecting roads It should be noted that there is a potential for washout of the subbase
course depending on the amount of surface water runoff along these access roads
Therefore some maintenance of these access roads should be expected
For connecting roads Ngkeklau Connecting Road with roadway gradients in

concrete over 150 mm of aggregate base course on 150 mm of subbase course be
provided for a more durable surface The shoulder areas for these steeper portions of the
connecting roads should be treated to reduce the potential for erosion and raveling of the
untreated shoulder areas due to the steeper connecting road gradients Similar to the
paved roadway areas we recommend that the shoulder areas of these connecting roads
be treated with 130 mm of cement treated subbase

asphalt concrete pavement on 150 mm of aggregate base course may be used for the
portion of the road where the gradient is greater than 8 percent Due to the rocky
subgrade conditions anticipated for the Ngaraard Connecting Road the subbase course
layer has been eliminated from the pavement section

N Subgrade Subbase and Base Preparation
In general we recommend that the pavement subgrades including shoulder and
gutter areas be scarified to a depth of about 200 mm moisture conditioned to at least
2 percent above the optimum moisture and compacted to a minimum of 90 percent
relative compaction prior to placement of the subbase course aggregate base course and
cement treated subbase layers Relative compaction refers to the in place dry density of
soil expressed as a percentage of the maximum dry density of the same soil established in
accordance with ASTM D 1557 91 or AASHTO T 180 test procedures using the moist
preparation method wet to dry Optimum moisture is the water content percentage by
dry weight corresponding to the maximum dry density
W O 3595 00 D BEELAEE ene Page 72
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
Where shrinkage cracks are noted after compaction of the subgrade we
recommend that the soil be prepared again as recommended above Saturation and
subsequent yielding of the exposed subgrade due to inclement weather and poor drainage
may require over excavation of the soft areas and replacement with well compacted
general fill Due to the generally wet conditions at the site we anticipate that the
Contractor may encounter pumping subgrade conditions during the pavement subgrade
preparation Where pumping conditions are encountered during compaction of the
subgrade soils the subgrade soils may be scarified allowed to dry by aeration and
prepared again as recommended above Considering the generally wet environment at
the site the pumping subgrade conditions may be stabilized by cement treatment As a
preliminary guide one sack of cement 42 kg may be used for approximately 4 square
meters of subgrade area This mix should be adjusted in the field based on the exposed
subgrade soil conditions
The subbase course may consist of sand and gravel mixtures including coralline
sands and gravels with a minimum CBR value of 25 corresponds to an R value of about
25 The subbase course should also be free from lumps clay balls and organic and
other deleterious materials In general the subbase course should be well graded from
coarse to fine with no particles larger than 75 mm in largest dimension In addition the
subbase material should contain not more than 15 percent particles less than
Aggregate base course should consist of crushed rock volcanic breccia or basalt
aggregate with a minimum CBR value of 85 corresponds to an R value of about 80
Aggregate base course should be well graded from coarse to fine with no particles larger
than 50 mm in largest dimension and should contain less than 10 percent particles less
than
Cement treated base should consist of Portland cement stabilized aggregate base
course The aggregate requirements for the cement treated base should conform to the
requirements for aggregate base course The cement treated base should be stabilized
with adequate cement content to obtain a minimum unconfined compressive strength of at
least based on a 7 day curing period
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
The cement treated subbase recommended for the shoulder areas should consist
of cement stabilized subbase material The cement treated subbase should be stabilized
with adequate cement content to obtain a minimum unconfined compressive strength of at
least based on a 7 day curing period
The aggregate base course and subbase course materials should be
moisture conditioned to above the optimum moisture content and compacted to at least
95 percent relative compaction in accordance with ASTM Test Designation D 1557 91
or AASHTO T 180 Cement treated base and cement treated subbase for shoulders
should be moisture conditioned to near the optimum moisture content and compacted to a
minimum of 95 percent relative compaction in accordance with ASTM Test Designation D
558 96 The maximum compacted thickness of each layer should not exceed 200 mm for
the cement treated base cement treated subbase aggregate base course and subbase
course CBR and field density tests should be performed on the actual subgrade soils
encountered during construction to confirm the adequacy of the pavement design
O Pavement Drainage System
One of the primary distress mechanisms observed in pavement structures is
pumping due to saturation of the base subbase and or subgrade soils Based on our
experience with projects having similar soil conditions there is a strong tendency for water
to accumulate at the interface between the granular subbase course and the underlying
clayey pavement subgrade soils In addition free water voids in the pavement section
repeated heavy wheel loads and an erodible base can also cause pumping conditions
Unless the pavement subgrade soils are properly prepared and adequate drainage is
provided the accumulated water will likely soften the silty and clayey subgrade soils and
cause premature failure of the pavement Therefore paved areas should be sloped and
drainage gradients maintained to carry all surface water off the site Surface water
ponding should not be allowed anywhere on the site during or after construction
In general the primary source of free water in the pavement structure is surface
water infiltration through cracks and joints in the pavement A major source of infiltrated
moisture is the longitudinal pavement shoulder interface Water also enters the pavement
structure from shallow ditches To reduce moisture infiltration into the pavement structure
W O 3595 00 D Page 74
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
and underlying subgrade soils all pavement cracks should be sealed Considering the
high rainfall in this area longitudinal edge drains should be considered along alignments
with relatively flat gradients and transverse drains should be provided at low spots along
the roadway alignment and ford crossings
As mentioned earlier pavement damage is generally caused by poor surface and
subsurface drainage conditions along the roadway alignment Therefore we recommend
that longitudinal edge drains along both sides of the roadway alignment be considered
where the roadway gradient is less than 2 percent Where needed a longitudinal edge
drain collector system with outlet pipes should be provided for positive drainage The
outlets must discharge into appropriate drainage outlet structures such as drainage
ditches culverts or catch basins Longitudinal edge drains will not be required for the
causeway pavement section provided that the asphalt concrete pavement and aggregate
base course extend to the outside edge of the shoulder
The typical edge drain should consist of a conventional edge subdrain
The conventional edge subdrain would consist of a perforated pipe with
perforations facing down surrounded by drain rock The invert of the edge drain
perforated pipe should be at least 200 mm below the bottom of the subbase course as
shown on Plate 13 A non woven geotextile such as Mirafi 180N or equivalent should be
placed around the permeable backfill material drain rock to reduce the potential for
migration of fines into the drainage material Care should be exercised when placing the
base subbase and or construction of the surface course to avoid damaging the edge
drain collector pipe As an alternative a geocomposite fin subdrain may be used as the
longitudinal edge drain as shown on Plate 13

A bridge structure is planned at the southern end of the proposed causeway
alignment between about and of the North Route The bridge
will be a two span bridge of approximately 70 meters long measured from abutment to
abutment The bridge will also be supported by a center pier at about
Our interpretation of the subsurface conditions at the bridge location is presented on the
generalized subsurface profile as shown on Plate 14 Descriptions and graphic
representations of the materials encountered in the drilled borings are provided on the
Logs of Borings in Appendix A
One of the most common cause of bridge failures stems from scouring of bridge
foundations from flood or other water flow damage Based on the information provided
we understand that the causeway channel floor and side slopes at the proposed bridge
structure will be lined with riprap as a counter measure against scour potential Therefore
consideration for the potential for scour at the north abutment and center pier locations will
not be necessary in the analyses for bridge foundations since counter measures against
scour potential have been implemented
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered and the foundation loads
provided we believe that the proposed causeway bridge located at the southern end of
the causeway embankment may be supported by a composite foundation system
consisting of shallow and deep foundations Based on the borings drilled the south
abutment and center pier may be supported by shallow foundations bearing on the very
dense volcanic breccia anticipated at relatively shallow depths For the north abutment
driven pile foundations socketed into the dense volcanic breccia underlying the site at
greater depths will be required for bearing and lateral support
Based on a design bottom of footing elevation of about 4 85 meters MSL for the
center pier we anticipate that substantial dewatering of the center pier footing excavation
will be required during construction Based on previous experience with similar site
conditions an interlocking sheet pile cofferdam has worked successfully in reducing the

amount of seepage water Predrilling into the volcanic breccia formation may be required
in order to socket the sheet piles into the underlying rock formation to serve as a cut off
wall

B Channel Material for Scour Analysis
As previously indicated the foundation design of the north abutment and center
pier will need to take into consideration the potential for channel scour Scour is the result
of erosive action of flowing water excavating and carrying away material from the bed and
banks of the channel Total scour over a period of time generally consists of
three components 1 Aggregation and Degradation 2 Contraction Scour and 3 Local
Scour The rates of scour depend on a number of factors such as the shape and
dimensions of a pier or abutment depth of flow velocity of approach flow size and
gradation of channel bed material and bed configuration
One of the factors affecting the scour depth is the grain size characteristics of the
channel bed material The median diameter of the channel bed material Dso in
conjunction with the depth of flow and flow velocity is used to calculate fall velocity of
channel bed materials in scour depth analysis In order to evaluate the size and gradation
of the channel bed material for scour depth analysis at the bridge abutment and center pier
locations soil samples from the drilled borings and bulk samples of the channel bed and or
bank materials were retrieved for our laboratory tests
The channel bed materials generally consist of coral sands and gravel lagoonal
deposits Sieve analyses with hydrometer tests were performed on the retrieved soil
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
samples to provide grain size distribution of the materials The results of our laboratory
tests are presented on Plates B 7 and B 8 of Appendix B and were provided to the project
Civil Engineer for the scour depth analyses Based on the results of our laboratory tests a
median diameter Dso of 2 mm for the channel bed material at the planned bridge
structure was selected for scour analyses
Scour analyses were performed by the project Civil Engineer for the 100 year storm
event only due to the lack of data for extrapolating to the 500 year storm event Due to the
substantial scour depths anticipated from the analyses counter measures against scour
potential were implemented to reduce the effects of scour on the bridge foundations
Therefore the foundation design recommendations presented in the following sections of
this report are based on the assumption that counter measures against scour potential
have been implemented at the planned bridge structure
C Shallow Foundations
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered in our drilled borings we believe
that the south abutment and center pier may be supported on shallow foundations bearing
on the very dense volcanic breccia encountered at depths of about 4 to 6 meters below
the existing ground or water surface The wing walls of the bridge extending back from the
bridge structure may also be supported on shallow foundations bearing on the dense
volcanic breccia The dense volcanic breccia encountered at or near the center pier
location may be considered as a non erodible surface based on a rock classification with
a Rock Quality Designation RQD of more than 50 percent and should not be affected by
SCOUT
Based on the Load and Resistance Factor Design LRFD methods for design of
highway bridges we believe that an ultimate bearing pressure of up to 900 kilopascals
kPa may be used to evaluate the extreme event limit state of the footings bearing on the
very dense volcanic breccia encountered at shallow depths The extreme event limit state
of the footings is generally due to unique occurrences such as seismic loading conditions
To evaluate the strength limit state of the foundations a bearing pressure of up to 540 kPa
may be used based on a resistance factor of 0 6
W O 3595 00 D Page 79
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
In general footings should be embedded a minimum of 500 mm below the lowest
adjacent finish grade or extended deeper to bear on the dense volcanic breccia formation
In addition where the footings will be affected by scour potential the footings should be
embedded below the scour elevation bear on a non erodible surface or be supported on
a deep foundation system In order for the center pier footing to bear on the dense
volcanic breccia as recommended we believe that an excavation on the order of about
3 to 4 meters below the ground surface mudline will be needed to expose the dense
volcanic breccia Considering that the center pier footing will extend to about 4 85 meters
MSL we recommend that the center pier footing be constructed within a sheet pile
cofferdam structure to provide a relatively dry excavation to work within At about
4 85 meters MSL we anticipate that the bottom of footing will likely encounter the dense
volcanic breccia formation However it may be necessary for the excavation to extend
deeper to locate the dense volcanic breccia formation Where there is a need to
over excavate the unsuitable materials to expose the dense volcanic breccia the
over excavation between the bottom of footing elevation and the dense volcanic breccia
may be backfilled with tremie concrete
If foundations are located next to utility trenches or easements they should be
embedded below a 1H 1V imaginary plane extending upward from the bottom edge of the
utility trench or as deep as the inverts of the utility lines This requirement is necessary to
avoid surcharging adjacent below grade structures with additional structural loads and to
reduce the potential for foundation settlement
Based on a service limit state bearing pressure of 300 kPa we estimate that footing
settlements for foundations bearing on the very dense volcanic breccia as recommended
herein to be less than 25 mm total with differential settlements on the order of about
15 mm
Lateral loads acting on the bridge structure may be resisted by frictional resistance
between the base of the footings and the bearing material and by passive earth pressure
developed against the near vertical faces of the embedded portion of footings
A coefficient of friction of 0 64 and 0 75 may be used for the shallow footings bearing on
W O 3595 00 D Page 80
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
the very dense volcanic breccia for the strength limit state and extreme event limit state
respectively
Resistance due to passive pressure for the extreme event limit state may be
calculated using an equivalent fluid pressure of 55 kilonewtons per square meter per
meter of depth KN m for footings embedded in the stiff in situ soils or compacted fill
materials for the extreme event limit state A reduced passive resistance value of
30 kN m should be used for footings embedded below the groundwater level if
applicable Unless covered by pavements or slabs the passive resistance in the upper
300 mm should be neglected A resistance factor of 0 5 may be applied to the passive
earth resistances provided above for evaluating the strength limit state of the foundations
Because of the potential for variation in the depth to the volcanic breccia formation
and the relatively high bearing pressures provided we recommend that an engineer from
Geolabs Hawaii be present at the site to confirm the foundation bearing conditions and the
required embedment depths prior to the placement of reinforcing steel or concrete

1 Predrilling
Based on the results of our field exploration it appears that the very dense
volcanic breccia underlying the causeway alignment is sloping down towards the
center of the bay from the north and south sides of the causeway In order to
facilitate the pile driving operations into the sloping rock formation and other
potential obstructions predrilling at the proposed pile locations for the north
abutment structure will be required prior to pile installation and should be
included in the contract documents Based on our field exploration we anticipate
that the predrilling depth should extend a minimum of 2 meters into the hard rock
formation or to about elevation 12 meters MSL for the north abutment structure
whichever is deeper The required predrilling depth should be verified and or
modified by Geolabs Hawaii during the test pile program depending on the
conditions encountered and pile driving equipment used
We wish to emphasize that the diameter of the predrilled holes should be limited
to less than about 800 mm to facilitate the pile driving operations to socket the
pile into the dense volcanic breccia The annular space between the piles and
predrilled holes should be backfilled with drain rock to reduce the potential for
migration of soils into the predrilled holes The upper 5 meters of the predrilled
holes below the bottom of pile cap elevation should be grouted with tremie
concrete after pile installation to provide the recommended lateral load capacity
2 Uplift Load Resistance
Uplift loads may be resisted by a combination of the dead weight of the driven
pile and by shear along the pile surface and the adjacent soils The
recommended uplift load capacities for the extreme event and strength limit
states are 150 and 75 kN respectively These uplift load capacities are for pile
groups therefore a group resistance factor of 0 55 has been applied to the
W O 3595 00 D Page 83
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
values provided The uplift load capacities provided already include the weight of
the pile The structural capacity of the pile member in tension should be checked
by the project Structural Engineer

4 Pile Foundation Settlements
Settlement of the pile foundations will result primarily from elastic compression of
the pile member and the subgrade response We estimate that the total settlement
of the pile supported foundation to be less than 15 mm with differential settlements
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
between piles not exceeding about one half that amount We believe that these
settlements are essentially elastic and should occur as the loads are applied
5 Pile Hammer Selection
The piles should be driven with a hammer capable of delivering a rated energy
between 150 000 and 200 000 newton meters The hammer should be equipped
with an energy control level such that driving within the loose soft soils may be
controlled at a lower energy to reduce tensile stress development within the piles
which could damage or cause cracking of the piles
Determination of driving equipment to be used for this project should take into
consideration the matching of the pile hammer with the weight of the piles to be
driven This will result in the pile hammer efficiently delivering the required energy
to the top of the pile and also reducing damage to the piles Geolabs Hawaii
should review the pile driving equipment proposed to evaluate if the equipment is
capable of installing the piles to the recommended capacity
The Contractor should forward pile and driving equipment data forms for review
prior to construction for evaluation Geolabs Hawaii should review the submittal
and assess the driveability of piles with the use of the selected hammer A wave
equation analysis should be performed after selection of the pile driving equipment
to assess the driveability of the piles and pile driving criteria Due to possible
variation in the length of the piles unit prices should be obtained during bidding for
add ons shorter piles etc
6 Pile Driving Criteria
For preliminary design of the 600 mm round precast prestressed concrete piles
pile driving may be terminated when the aggregated blow counts are at least
12 blows per 25 mm for the last 500 mm or 18 blows per 25 mm for the last
100 mm The preliminary driving criteria should be confirmed and or modified after
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
selection of the pile driving equipment and performance of a wave equation
analysis In addition the pile driving criteria may be modified during the test pile
driving to accommodate variations of soil conditions in the field
Each pile should be driven continuously without interruption A pile may be rejected
or its design capacity may be reduced when the driving resistance is interrupted for
more than 4 hours and the pile cannot be driven to the required depth During soft
driving such as when the pile tip is within soft loose soils the hammer stroke
or energy should be carefully controlled such that significant tensile stresses do
not develop within the pile
Because variations in soil strength characteristics are likely to exist in the bearing
soils the supporting capacity of each pile should be confirmed in the field by a
representative from our office during driving It should be noted that the pile design
was developed from our pile analysis using the field exploration data A test pile
program should be carried out to confirm our preliminary pile design and establish
the driving criteria The production pile driving criteria may be revised based on the
test pile program
ie Test Pile Program
We recommend that a test pile program be conducted for the proposed bridge
foundation system All test piles should be instrumented for monitoring by a pile
driving analyzer PDA to observe the stresses developed in the test piles and to
evaluate pile bearing capacity In addition the test pile program should also include
at least one load test to fulfill the following objectives
e Examine the driveability of piles with the use of the selected hammer
e Estimate the production pile lengths
e Assess the test pile driving criteria based on a dynamic analysis of the pile
using a pile driving analyzer which may be modified for use as the
production pile driving criteria
To achieve these objectives we recommend that the test pile program consist of a
minimum of 3 piles for the north abutment structure The test piles may be
incorporated into the actual foundation system upon the evaluation of the load test
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
results Test piles should be cast at least 3 meters longer than the estimated pile
length to account for potential variation in subsurface conditions In addition the
test pile program should provide flexibility to allow for addition or relocation of the
test piles as may be deemed necessary by our office to suit the conditions
encountered during the test pile driving Restriking of all the test piles will be
required as part of the test pile program
The pile load test should be performed in general accordance with ASTM Test
Designation D 1143 by loading the test pile to the ultimate load capacity for the
single pile condition We recommend that a representative from Geolabs Hawaii
be present during all pile driving operations to observe the actual driving behavior
and to further evaluate the field performance It should be noted that the pile
design and driving criteria recommended were developed from our pile analysis
using the field exploration data Therefore observation of the pile
driving operations by Geolabs Hawaii is necessary to confirm our design
assumptions It is also recommended that the production pile driving be observed
by Geolabs Hawaii so that driving resistances tip elevations and axial load bearing
capacity may be confirmed
E Abutment Fills
Based on the existing grades and the design finish grades at the bridge abutment
locations we envision that new fills on the order of about 3 and 6 meters will be required
for construction of the south and north abutments of the Ngaraard Causeway Bridge
structure Placement of the approximately 6 meters of fill for the north abutment will
induce consolidation of the soft loose lagoonal deposits in the bay As indicated in Section
V of this report complete removal and replacement of the soft soil deposits or the use of
stage construction may be required at the north abutment where the new fills exceed
about 5 meters in thickness Since the south abutment is located over dense volcanic
breccia we do not envision that placement of the approximately 3 meters of fill at the
south abutment will need special requirements such as a settlement waiting period
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
In general it is our opinion that placement of the causeway fills at the north
abutment should be initiated and constructed to the finished grades as early as possible to
allow the existing ground to adjust to the new fill loads A settlement monitoring program
should be implemented to evaluate the magnitude and rate of the estimated settlements
during placement of the bridge abutment fills Detailed information and recommendations
pertaining to settlement monitoring and settlement waiting period for the causeway
embankment are presented in Section V Ground Settlement section of this report After
completion of the fill placement and observation of the settlement waiting period the north
abutment should be excavated down to the bottom of footing elevation prior to the driven
pile installation
Abutment fill slopes constructed of granular fills may be designed with inclinations
of or flatter provided that adequate slope protection are provided to reduce the
potential for erosion The fill slopes should be over built and cut back to the final
configuration to provide adequate compaction at the edge of the slope face Discussions
on the construction of the other portions of the causeway embankment have been
presented in Section V Causeway Fill section of this report
F Structure Approach Slabs
As previously indicated placement of new fills at the abutment locations are
planned Placement of new fills at the north abutment will induce consolidation of the
soft loose lagoonal deposits at the north abutment location Settlements on the order of
about 500 to 1000 mm due to placement of up to 6 meters of fills have been estimated for
the north abutment Post construction settlements of the abutment fills are estimated to be
between 25 and 50 mm As a result of these anticipated post construction settlements we
recommend that structure approach slabs be provided at the abutment locations to reduce
the potential for abrupt differential settlements between the pile supported north abutment
structure and the abutment fill The approach slab should also be provided for the south
abutment to reduce the potential for abrupt differential settlements between the abutment
structure supported on the dense volcanic breccia and the abutment fills
The structure approach slab should be supported on a minimum of 200 mm of
subbase course placed on a prepared subgrade The subgrade should be scarified to a
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
depth of about 200 mm moisture conditioned to at least 2 percent above the optimum
moisture content and compacted to not less than 90 percent relative compaction The
subbase course should be moisture conditioned to above the optimum moisture content
and compacted to at least 95 percent relative compaction
G Retaining Structures
All retaining structures including abutment structures retaining walls wing walls
box culverts etc should be designed to resist the lateral earth pressures due to the
adjacent soils and surcharge effects The recommended lateral earth pressures for design
of retaining structures expressed in equivalent fluid pressures are presented below
Design of foundations for these retaining structures are discussed in the Box Culverts
Wing Walls and Retaining Walls section of this report

Backfill behind retaining structures may consist of the recompacted on site soils
general fill compacted to between 85 and 90 percent relative compaction for retaining
structures designed based on the general fill soils For retaining structures designed
based on granular fill soils such as the abutments for the causeway bridge granular fill
material compacted to between 90 and 95 percent relative compaction should be used
Over compaction of the retaining structure backfill should be avoided
In general an active condition may be used for gravity walls or walls that are free to
deflect by as much as 0 5 percent of the wall height If the tops of the walls are not free to
deflect beyond this degree or are restrained the walls should be designed for the at rest
condition These lateral earth pressures do not include hydrostatic pressures therefore it
is imperative that a proper subdrainage system be incorporated in the construction details
Retaining walls should be well drained to reduce the build up of hydrostatic
pressures A typical drainage system would consist of a 300 mm wide zone of drain rock
immediately around a perforated pipe perforations down at the base of the wall
discharging to an appropriate outlet or weepholes As an alternative a prefabricated
drainage product such as MiraDrain or EnkaDrain may be used in lieu of the drainage
material The prefabricated drainage product should also be hydraulically connected to a
perforated pipe at the base of the wall which is daylighted for free drainage Backfill
behind the permeable drainage zone may consist of general fill or granular fill material
depending on the design parameters used Unless covered by concrete slabs or
W O 3595 00 D erent Page 90
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
pavements the upper 300 mm of backfill should consist of low expansion impervious
material general fill to reduce water infiltration behind the walls
Surcharge stresses due to areal surcharges line loads and point loads within a
horizontal distance equal to the depth of the wall should be considered in the design For
uniform surcharge stresses imposed on the loaded side of the wall a rectangular
distribution with uniform pressure equal to the following percentage active or at rest
conditions of the vertical surcharge pressure acting on the entire height of the wall may be
used in design Additional analyses during design may be needed to evaluate the
surcharge effects of point loads and line loads

H Box Culverts Wing Walls and Retaining Walls
We envision that reinforced concrete box culverts with wing walls will be required
for the circulation culverts in the causeway embankment for the proposed roadway project
In addition we understand that some retaining walls for grade separation will be
constructed for this project In general we anticipate that shallow foundations bearing on
the stiff saprolite soils encountered at the project site or the recommended working
platform or stabilization layer for soft ground stabilization may be utilized for support of
the planned reinforced concrete box culvert wing wall structures and retaining walls
Based on the field exploration results we believe that the following values may be used to
evaluate the bearing support sliding resistance and passive pressure resistance of the
planned structures based on LRFD methods
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
Bearing

kPa

kN m
A 200 mm gravel cushion layer should be provided between the bottom of the box
culvert and the underlying saprolite soils to provide more uniform bearing support
The gravel cushion layer should consist of drain rock A schematic detail of the cushion
layer is shown on Plate 15 Where the box culverts will be subjected to heavy overburden
pressures due to high embankment fills we recommend that the thickness of the gravel
cushion layer be increased in accordance with the guidelines provided on Plate 15
Based on a service limit state bearing pressure of 150 kPa we estimate that
foundation settlements under the anticipated design loads for foundations bearing on the
stiff saprolite soils to be less than 25 mm
In general foundations should be embedded a minimum of 500 mm below the
lowest adjacent finish grades If foundations are located next to utility trenches or
easements they should be embedded below a 1H 1V imaginary plane extending upward
from the bottom edge of the utility trench or as deep as the inverts of the utility lines This
requirement is necessary to avoid surcharging adjacent below grade structures with
additional structural loads and to reduce the potential for foundation settlement
Lateral loads acting on the structure may be resisted by frictional resistance
between the base of the foundation and the bearing soil and by passive earth pressure
developed against the near vertical faces of the embedded portion of the foundation The
values presented in the table above may be used to evaluate the passive pressure
resistance for footings embedded and bearing on the stiff saprolite soils A reduced value
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
of 25 kN m extreme even limit state should be used for foundations embedded below
the groundwater level Unless covered by pavements or slabs the passive resistance in
the upper 300 mm should be neglected
Pipe Culverts
Numerous pipe culverts are planned for this project In general a granular bedding
consisting of 150 mm of drain rock is recommended under pipe culverts If soft and or
loose soils are encountered at the proposed pipe inverts an additional 500 mm of drain
rock wrapped in a non woven geotextile such as Mirafi 180N or equivalent should be
provided below the bedding layer for more uniform support Drain rock should also be
used for the initial backfill up to the springline of the pipe 0 5 times the pipe diameter to
provide adequate support around the pipes For larger pipe culverts exceeding 600 mm in
diameter we believe that the initial backfill may be reduced to 0 25 times the outside pipe
diameter measured from the top of the bedding layer as shown on Plate 16
Where groundwater is present above the pipe drain rock should be used as backfill
up to at least 200 mm above the water level It is critical that free draining materials be
used for the initial backfill to reduce the potential for formation of voids below the haunches
of pipes and to provide adequate support for the sides of the pipes which could result in
settlement of the backfill and damage to the pipes The use of sand as backfill materials
around the pipe culverts should be prohibited due to the potential for migration or loss of
fines during heavy rains which may result in potential ground settlement and pavement
distress
The upper portion of the trench backfill from the level of the springline of the pipes
or above the initial backfill level as recommended for larger diameter pipes to the top of
the subgrade or finished grade may consist of general fill with a maximum particle size of
less than 150 mm The backfill material should be moisture conditioned to at least
2 percent above the optimum moisture placed in maximum 200 mm level loose lifts and
mechanically compacted to not less than 85 percent relative compaction If the pipe
culverts are backfilled in a trench after completion of the grading operations we
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
recommend that the upper 1 meter of the trench backfill below the pavement subgrade be
compacted to at least 90 percent relative compaction A typical section of the trench
details is presented on Plate 16
For the reinforced concrete pipe culverts a minimum cover of 0 75 meters should
be maintained above the top of the pipes to the finish grades in order to facilitate
construction of the pavement structure Where the longitudinal gradients of the reinforced
concrete pipe culverts are 20 percent or steeper the lower three pipe segments of the
concrete pipe should be supported on a concrete cradle near the outlet end of the culvert
to anchor the pipe culverts and to restrain the culverts from lateral movement due to
potential scour The outlet wall structure should also be properly keyed into stiff soils to
anchor the pipes A typical section of the concrete cradle is provided on Plate 17
The concrete cradle may also be used in areas where substantial fills are placed over the
culverts to increase the bedding factors
J Soft Soil Stabilization Under Pipe and Box Culverts
Soft and wet soils will be encountered at the foundation subgrade levels at some of
the drainage crossings Some of these potential areas have been presented in Section V
Soft Ground Stabilization section of this report In areas where the drainage structures
are underlain by relatively thin soft soil deposits on the order of 1 to 2 meters below the
bottom of the drainage structure elevation we recommend that these soft and wet soils
be removed to expose the underlying firm soils prior to backfilling with suitable fills and
construction of the drainage structures We recommend that contract documents include
additive and deductive unit prices for over excavation to remove soft soils and suitable fill
placement as a contingency item to account for variations in the over excavation
quantities
For drainage structures underlain by significant soft soil deposits but with relatively
thin fills fills less than about 5 meters thick we believe that the fill embankment may be
constructed with a temporary drainage structure followed by a settlement waiting period to
allow the anticipated settlements to occur After the settlements have occurred and are
near equilibrium the temporary drainage structure may be excavated and removed
W O 3595 00 D Page 94
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
followed by construction of the permanent drainage structure For the box culverts we
recommend that a 1 2 meter thick layer of drain rock wrapped in a woven geotextile Mirafi
600X or equivalent with 1200 or equivalent be incorporated into the
stabilization layer to improve the bearing capacity of the soft soils as shown on Plate 15
Due to the large widths of the box culverts a relatively thick stabilization layer with soil
reinforcements is recommended to provide a firm foundation for the box culverts For the
pipe culverts a stabilization layer consisting of 500 mm of drain rock wrapped in a
non woven geotextile Mirafi 180N or equivalent should be provided below the cushion
layer for more uniform support as shown on Plate 16 and discussed in the Pipe Culverts
section of this report
In areas where the drainage structures are underlain by significant soft soil deposits
and substantial fills greater than 5 meters thick we believe that the permanent drainage
structures located in these soft soil areas will be subjected to a substantial amount of
settlements resulting from consolidation of the soft soil deposits under the substantial fill
loads In order to reduce the effects of the substantial settlements on the drainage
structures we recommend that the soft soil deposits underneath these drainage structures
be removed and replaced with suitable fill materials prior to their installation The
approximate lateral limits of the soft soil removal and suitable fill replacement are shown
on Plate 11 After installation of the drainage structures stage construction may then
proceed for these soft soil areas with substantial fill loads as discussed in Section V
Special Earthwork Requirements section of this report
K Waterlines
We understand that new waterlines relocation of existing waterlines will be
installed along portions of the proposed roadway alignments as part of the project
construction These new waterlines will be 150 mm in diameter and will consist of PVC
pipes In addition we envision that water laterals 50 mm connecting the waterlines to
service the existing homes will be required In general the following guidelines may be
used for installation of the waterlines for the proposed roadway project
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
The stress distribution against the bottom of a PVC pipe has a significant effect on
the load supporting capacity of the pipe Therefore the pipe bedding is an important
design consideration For waterlines bearing on the in situ clayey silt soils a minimum of
150 mm of bedding material should be provided below the pipe The bedding may be
reduced to 100 mm for the water laterals 50 mm diameter For pipes with inverts near
the soft loose soils a stabilization layer consisting of 500 mm of drain rock wrapped in a
non woven geotextile should be provided below the bedding layer for more uniform
support The bedding material should consist of select granular material free from rocks
25 mm or larger in any dimension or free from rocks of such size as recommended by the
pipe manufacturer whichever is smaller In addition the granular bedding material should
be free draining and should not have more than 10 percent particles less than
In general the initial backfill should consist of select granular material similar to the
bedding layer extending up to at least 300 mm above the top of the pipes It is critical that
a free draining granular material be used to reduce the potential for formation of voids
below the haunches of pipes and to provide adequate support for the sides of the pipes
The initial backfill should be densified with the use of a vibratory plate or similar light
compaction equipment The use of on site clayey soils as initial backfill immediately
around pipes is not recommended
The final backfill extending from 300 mm above the top of the pipes to the
pavement subgrades may consist of general fill material The backfill should be
moisture conditioned to above the optimum moisture content placed in level lifts on the
order of about 150 to 200 mm in loose thickness and compacted to at least 90 percent
relative compaction
Due to the proximity of the waterline pipe to the finished subgrades we recommend
that a minimum cover of 900 mm be maintained between the top of the waterline pipe and
the finished pavement grade for waterline pipes constructed within the pavement areas
The Contractor should exercise care when preparing the subgrade for the pavement
above a waterline trench to avoid crushing the PVC pipe from the weight of the
construction equipment
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
L Shoring
We understand that excavations may be required for construction of some of the
drainage structures and bridge foundations Open cut excavation may be desirable
provided that the excavation may be setback away from existing on grade and
below grade structures and utility lines Based on the boring information we believe that
temporary cut slopes on the order of about or flatter may be used for open cut
excavations in the stiff saprolite soils anticipated at the project site Temporary open cut
excavations in the granular causeway fills should be made with slopes of about
or flatter
Open cut excavations greater than 2 meters deep in the soft and or loose
lagoonal soils swamp and marsh deposits are not recommended and should be shored
Excavations into the soft subsoils should be made only with proper shoring installation
Therefore we recommend that the excavation to construct the center pier footing be
shored One possible shoring system is to use a sheet pile cofferdam to construct the
center pier footing It should be noted that the bottom of the center pier footing excavation
will encounter dense volcanic breccia formation and will not readily provide toe
embedment for the sheet pile cofferdam Therefore other means of providing the passive
pressures needed at the toe of the sheet pile cofferdam such as a tremie concrete plug
will be necessary The Contractor should be responsible for the details of the shoring
system In addition the excavated soils should be stockpiled no closer than a horizontal
distance equal to the depth of the excavation from the edge of the excavation to reduce
the potential for ground movement
Where an open cut excavation is not possible the excavation for the construction
of the structures will need to be adequately shored to reduce the potential for adjacent
ground movement The excavation support and shoring system used must comply with all
applicable safety requirements One possible method of shoring would be to use sheet
pile shoring It should be noted that during and after the installation of sheet piles minor
settlements may be possible Therefore it is recommended that a qualified geotechnical
engineer be retained by the Contractor to design and evaluate the shoring system used
It should also be noted that some areas may expose the dense volcanic breccia rock
W O 3595 00 D Page 97
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
formation and or boulders in the planned excavation therefore use of a sheet pile shoring
system may not be appropriate in these subsurface conditions
The adequacy and safety of the shoring installation should be made the sole
responsibility of the Contractor His her representative who should be required to be
continuously present on site during excavation and construction work will have the best
opportunity to promptly observe any changing conditions during construction such as
unforeseen subsoils unexpectedly high groundwater table inappropriate construction
sequence or techniques etc which may affect shoring stability
However it must be noted that some minor movements of the shoring system and
the adjacent ground may still occur due to changes in earth stresses during excavation
Due to the complexity of the stress changes it is difficult to accurately estimate the
magnitude of movement The magnitude also depends greatly upon workmanship such
as how quickly and tightly the shoring and bracing supports are installed on the subsoil
conditions the size of the excavation and the rate of excavation Therefore it is important
to realize that the excavation shoring should be installed properly and as early as practical
if necessary and that the adjacent ground should be continuously monitored for cracks
dips and or other indications of movements with instruments
M Dewatering
Dewatering of some excavations will be necessary where the planned excavation
extends below the existing groundwater level Therefore we recommend that dewatering
provisions be included in the contract documents for construction Since the excavation
will involve discharge of groundwater a permit may be necessary The Contractor should
consult their independent consultant or the Republic of Palau Environmental Quality
Protection Board EQPB for the latest regulations and information pertaining to any
required permit application
Dewatering for construction is the responsibility of the Contractor The selection of
equipment and methods of dewatering should be left up to the Contractor and he she
should be aware that modifications to the dewatering system may be required during
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
construction depending on the conditions encountered The dewatering method selected
should have minimal impact on the groundwater level surrounding the proposed
excavation
It is our opinion that the definition of Dewatering in the contract documents should
be written to include all works or systems required to lower the natural groundwater table
and or to exclude the water from the excavations to allow construction of the proposed
structures under safe and dry conditions These works or systems may include but are
not limited to well pumping grouting cut off walls tremie concrete plugs or any
combination of the above and or other possible methods
1 Dewatering Considerations
We suggest that the following three basic criteria be considered in selection of a
suitable method of dewatering
e The dewatering method should result in the least disturbance or damage to
existing structures roads and environment
e The dewatering method should maintain stability of and also provide safe
and dry working conditions in the excavation
e The dewatering method should be sufficiently flexible to allow modifications
to accommodate various ground conditions
2 Dewatering Precaution and Monitoring
The potential impact of the dewatering system selected on depressing the natural
groundwater table must be carefully evaluated by the Contractor prior to
dewatering It is recommended that a qualified geotechnical engineer be retained
by the Contractor to design and evaluate the dewatering system used The impact
and safety of the dewatering operations should be made the sole responsibility of
the Contractor His her qualified representative who should be required to be
continuously present on site during dewatering activities will have the best
opportunity to promptly observe the effects of dewatering during construction and to
implement as soon as possible necessary precautionary or remedial measures
including but not limited to stopping or slowing down the dewatering operations
W O 3595 00 D Page 99
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
Where encountered at the bottom of excavations permeable granular subsoils may
be susceptible to piping and quick conditions The dewatering operations should
be carried out without creating a quick condition or softening at the bottom of
excavation Therefore the project dewatering operations should be done without
pumping out soil fines pumping clear water only and should be coordinated with
shoring installation such that the excavation stability is not adversely affected
Excessive pumping which removes soil fines may result in a blowing or heaving
of the excavation bottom or sides
END OF DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS

It is recommended that preliminary and final drawings and specifications for the

B Construction Observation
It is recommended that Geolabs Hawaii be retained to provide geotechnical
engineering services during the construction of this project The items of construction
monitoring that are critical which require Special Inspection include the following
Removal of soft and or loose soils
Soft soil stabilization measures
Subgrade preparation
Fill placement and compaction testing
Review of settlement data
Review of reinforced earth slope submittals
Review of shoring and dewatering submittals
Observation of footing excavations and
Observation of test pile and production pile installation
Other aspects of earthwork construction should also be observed by a
representative from Geolabs Hawaii This is to observe compliance with the design
concepts specifications or recommendations and to expedite suggestions for design
changes that may be required in the event that subsurface conditions differ from those
anticipated at the time this report was prepared Geolabs Hawaii should be accorded the
opportunity to provide construction observation services to confirm the assumptions made
in formulating the recommendations presented in this report
W O 3595 00 D EOL ABS AVIAN Page 101
SECTION VII POST DESIGN SERVICES SERVICES DURING CONSTRUCTION
If the actual exposed subsurface conditions encountered during construction differ
from those assumed or considered in this report Geolabs Hawaii should be contacted to
review and or revise the geotechnical engineering recommendations presented herein
Any import fill material required should be tested for conformance with the project
specifications prior to hauling to the project site
We understand that Geolabs Hawaii may not be retained to provide on site
construction observation during the foundation and earthwork operations due to the locality
of the project site from our office We understand that a construction quality control CQC
representative will be retained for the project He she should be experienced in
construction observation and testing of earthwork operations The following minimum
requirements should be used to select CQC representatives
4 CQC Representative
The CQC Representative shall be a graduate engineer or a graduate of an
equivalent construction oriented college program with a minimum of five years
construction experience on similar type construction to this project including at
least two years of experience in quality control
2 Alternate CQC Representative
As part of the CQC staff the contractor shall designate an alternate to act for the
CQC Representative in the event that the CQC Representative is absent from
the construction site The alternate may not act for the CQC Representative for a
period longer than 30 consecutive calendar days The alternate
CQC Representative shall either be a graduate engineer or a graduate of an
equivalent construction oriented college program with a minimum one year of
quality control experience or a person with a minimum three years of
construction experience on similar type construction to the project and including
at least one year of quality control experience
END OF POST DESIGN SERVICES SERVICES DURING CONSTRUCTION
SECTION VIil LIMITATIONS
The findings analyses and recommendations submitted in this report are based
upon the information obtained through our literature research past project experience
site reconnaissance and field exploration performed for the proposed Palau Compact
Road Package D project Variations of conditions between and beyond the field
borings are likely to occur considering the long linear feature of the roadway alignment
Therefore the nature and extent of these variations may not become evident until
construction is underway If variations then appear evident it will be necessary to
reevaluate the findings and recommendations provided in this report
The locations of the field borings indicated in this report are approximate having
been estimated from the survey stakes placed along the alignments by R M Towill
Corporation and by taping from reference points and visible features shown on the
Preliminary Road Alignment Plans dated January 1997 and Plans for Construction of a
Portion of Palau Compact Road Package D dated prepared by R M
Towill Corporation The maps were used in conjunction with field compass readings
distance relationships from known features topography altimeter and Global Positioning
System equipment to obtain the approximate boring locations as shown on the Site Plans
Elevations of the borings were estimated from the contour lines shown on the same plans
borings should be considered accurate only to the degree implied by the methods used
The stratification breaks shown on the graphic representations of the borings depict
the approximate boundaries between soil rock types and as such may denote a gradual
transition Water level data from the borings were measured at the times shown on the
graphic representations and or presented in the text of this report These data have been
reviewed and interpretations made in the formulation of this report However it must be
noted that fluctuation may occur due to variation in rainfall tidal fluctuation time of year
temperature and other factors
W O 3595 00 D Page 103
GEOLABS HAWAII

This report has been prepared for the exclusive use of R M Towill Corporation
their Client the U S Army Corps of Engineers and other design subconsultants for
